<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Sleep</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Naturally recurring state of mind and body</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about sleep in humans. For non-human sleep, see <a href="/wiki/Sleep_in_animals" title="Sleep in animals">Sleep in animals</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sleep_(disambiguation)" title="Sleep (disambiguation)">Sleep (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Asleep" and "Slept" redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Asleep_(disambiguation)" title="Asleep (disambiguation)">Asleep (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SLEPT_analysis" title="SLEPT analysis">SLEPT analysis</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg"><img alt="A sleeping girl" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="777" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg/300px-Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg/450px-Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg/600px-Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Domenico_Fetti_-_Sleeping_Girl_-_WGA7863.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Sleeping Girl</i>, <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Fetti" title="Domenico Fetti">Domenico Fetti</a>, circa 1615</div></div></div>
<p><b>Sleep</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">naturally</a> recurring state of mind and body, characterized by altered <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>, relatively inhibited sensory activity, reduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> activity and inhibition of nearly all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voluntary_muscle" title="Voluntary muscle">voluntary muscles</a> during <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">rapid eye movement</a> (REM) sleep,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">[1]</a></sup> and reduced interactions with surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ninds_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ninds-2">[2]</a></sup> It is distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Wakefulness" title="Wakefulness">wakefulness</a> by a decreased ability to react to <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimuli</a>, but more reactive than a <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a> or <a href="/wiki/Disorders_of_consciousness" title="Disorders of consciousness">disorders of consciousness</a>, with sleep displaying different, active brain patterns.
</p><p>Sleep occurs in <a href="/wiki/Sleep_cycle" title="Sleep cycle">repeating periods</a>, in which the body alternates between two distinct modes: REM sleep and <a href="/wiki/Non-rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Non-rapid eye movement sleep">non-REM</a> sleep. Although REM stands for "rapid eye movement", this mode of sleep has many other aspects, including virtual <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a> of the body. A well-known feature of sleep is the <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dream</a>, an experience typically recounted in <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a> form, which resembles waking life while in progress, but which usually can later be distinguished as fantasy. During sleep, most of the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">body's systems</a> are in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabolic" title="Anabolic">anabolic</a> state, helping to restore the <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immune</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Muscular_system" title="Muscular system">muscular</a> systems;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krueger_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-krueger-3">[3]</a></sup> these are vital processes that maintain mood, memory, and cognitive function, and play a large role in the function of the <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-4">[4]</a></sup> The internal <a href="/wiki/Circadian_clock" title="Circadian clock">circadian clock</a> promotes sleep daily at night. The diverse purposes and mechanisms of sleep are the subject of substantial ongoing research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-5">[5]</a></sup> Sleep is a highly <a href="/wiki/Conserved_sequence" title="Conserved sequence">conserved</a> behavior across animal evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joiner2016_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joiner2016-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans may suffer from various <a href="/wiki/Sleep_disorder" title="Sleep disorder">sleep disorders</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Dyssomnia" title="Dyssomnia">dyssomnias</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypersomnia" title="Hypersomnia">hypersomnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narcolepsy" title="Narcolepsy">narcolepsy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sleep_apnea" title="Sleep apnea">sleep apnea</a>; <a href="/wiki/Parasomnia" title="Parasomnia">parasomnias</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sleepwalking" title="Sleepwalking">sleepwalking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep_behavior_disorder" title="Rapid eye movement sleep behavior disorder">rapid eye movement sleep behavior disorder</a>; <a href="/wiki/Bruxism" title="Bruxism">bruxism</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm_sleep_disorder" title="Circadian rhythm sleep disorder">circadian rhythm sleep disorders</a>. The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_light" title="Artificial light">artificial light</a> has substantially altered humanity's sleep patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Common sources of artificial light include the screens of electronic devices such as <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a> and televisions, which emit large amounts of blue light, a form of light typically associated with daytime. This disrupts the release of the hormone <a href="/wiki/Melatonin" title="Melatonin">melatonin</a> needed to regulate the sleep-cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience_of_sleep" title="Neuroscience of sleep">Neuroscience of sleep</a></div>
<p>The most pronounced physiological changes in sleep occur in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaquetEtAl2006_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaquetEtAl2006-9">[9]</a></sup> The brain uses significantly less energy during sleep than it does when awake, especially during non-REM sleep. In areas with reduced activity, the brain restores its supply of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the molecule used for short-term storage and transport of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In quiet waking, the brain is responsible for 20% of the body's energy use, thus this reduction has a noticeable effect on overall energy consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegel2008_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siegel2008-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep increases the <a href="/wiki/Sensory_threshold" title="Sensory threshold">sensory threshold</a>. In other words, sleeping persons perceive fewer stimuli, but can generally still respond to loud noises and other salient sensory events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegel2008_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Siegel2008-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaquetEtAl2006_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-MaquetEtAl2006-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep</a>, humans secrete bursts of <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormone</a>. All sleep, even during the day, is associated with the secretion of <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">prolactin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanCauterSpiegel1999_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanCauterSpiegel1999-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Key physiological methods for monitoring and measuring changes during sleep include <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">electroencephalography</a> (EEG) of <a href="/wiki/Neural_oscillation" title="Neural oscillation">brain waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrooculography" title="Electrooculography">electrooculography</a> (EOG) of eye movements, and <a href="/wiki/Electromyography" title="Electromyography">electromyography</a> (EMG) of <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a> activity. Simultaneous collection of these measurements is called <a href="/wiki/Polysomnography" title="Polysomnography">polysomnography</a>, and can be performed in a specialized <a href="/wiki/Sleep_medicine#Diagnostic_methods" title="Sleep medicine">sleep laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peraita-Adrados2005_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peraita-Adrados2005-14">[14]</a></sup> Sleep researchers also use simplified <a href="/wiki/Electrocardiography" title="Electrocardiography">electrocardiography</a> (EKG) for cardiac activity and <a href="/wiki/Actigraphy" title="Actigraphy">actigraphy</a> for motor movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peraita-Adrados2005_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peraita-Adrados2005-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brain_waves_in_sleep">Brain waves in sleep</span></h3>
<p>The electrical activity seen on an EEG is called brain waves. It has been revealed that the intensity of EEG waves on a frequency is related to one's sleep-wake cycle. Meaning that the body is linked to certain waves at certain points during a person's day such as; being asleep, falling asleep, being awake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The model EEG waveforms seen are; Alpha waves, beta waves, theta waves, gamma waves, and delta waves are all seen in the different stages of sleep. Each different wave maintains a different frequency and amplitude. Alpha waves are seen when a person is in a resting state, but is still fully conscious. Their eyes may be closed and all of their body is resting and  relatively still, where the body is starting to slow down. Beta waves take over alpha waves when a person is at attention, as they might be completing a task or concentrating on something. Beta waves consist of the highest of frequencies and the lowest of amplitude, at this point a person is fully alert. Alpha and beta waves are the only waves seen when a person is in an awake state. Gamma waves are seen when a person is highly focused on a task or using all their concentration.   Theta waves occur during the period of a person being awake, and they continue to transition into Stage 1 of sleep and in stage 2. Delta waves are seen in stages 3 and 4 of sleep when a person is in their deepest of sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-REM_and_REM_sleep">Non-REM and REM sleep</span></h3>
<p>Sleep is divided into two broad types: <a href="/wiki/Non-rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Non-rapid eye movement sleep">non-rapid eye movement</a> (non-REM or NREM) sleep and <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">rapid eye movement</a> (REM) sleep. Non-REM and REM sleep are so different that physiologists identify them as distinct behavioral states. Non-REM sleep occurs first and after a transitional period is called <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep</a> or deep sleep. During this phase, body temperature and heart rate fall, and the brain uses less energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaquetEtAl2006_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-MaquetEtAl2006-9">[9]</a></sup> REM sleep, also known as paradoxical sleep, represents a smaller portion of total sleep time. It is the main occasion for <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dreams</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Nightmare" title="Nightmare">nightmares</a>), and is associated with desynchronized and fast brain waves, eye movements, loss of muscle tone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ninds_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ninds-2">[2]</a></sup> and suspension of <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sleep_cycle" title="Sleep cycle">sleep cycle</a> of alternate NREM and REM sleep takes an average of 90 minutes, occurring 4–6 times in a good night's sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peraita-Adrados2005_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peraita-Adrados2005-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarley2007_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCarley2007-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Sleep_Medicine" title="American Academy of Sleep Medicine">American Academy of Sleep Medicine</a> (AASM) divides NREM into three stages: N1, N2, and N3, the last of which is also called delta sleep or <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The whole period normally proceeds in the order: N1 → N2 → N3 → N2 → REM. REM sleep occurs as a person returns to stage 2 or 1 from a deep sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ninds_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-ninds-2">[2]</a></sup> There is a greater amount of deep sleep (stage N3) earlier in the night, while the proportion of REM sleep increases in the two cycles just before natural awakening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peraita-Adrados2005_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Peraita-Adrados2005-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awakening">Awakening</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Waking up" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Waking_up_(disambiguation)" title="Waking up (disambiguation)">Waking up (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wakefulness" title="Wakefulness">Wakefulness</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascending_reticular_activating_system" title="Ascending reticular activating system">Ascending reticular activating system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1900_The_Awakening.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/1900_The_Awakening.jpg/220px-1900_The_Awakening.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/1900_The_Awakening.jpg/330px-1900_The_Awakening.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/1900_The_Awakening.jpg/440px-1900_The_Awakening.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1900_The_Awakening.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Awakening", an illustration to writing by <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a></div></div></div>
<p>Awakening can mean the end of sleep, or simply a moment to survey the environment and readjust body position before falling back asleep. Sleepers typically awaken soon after the end of a REM phase or sometimes in the middle of REM. Internal <a href="/wiki/Circadian_clock" title="Circadian clock">circadian indicators</a>, along with a successful reduction of homeostatic sleep need, typically bring about awakening and the end of the sleep cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AkerstedtEtAl2002_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AkerstedtEtAl2002-20">[20]</a></sup> Awakening involves heightened electrical activation in the brain, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Thalamus" title="Thalamus">thalamus</a> and spreading throughout the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cortex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AkerstedtEtAl2002_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-AkerstedtEtAl2002-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>During a night's sleep, a small amount of time is usually spent in a waking state. As measured by electroencephalography, young females are awake for 0–1% of the larger sleeping period; young males are awake for 0–2%. In adults, wakefulness increases, especially in later cycles. One study found 3% awake time in the first ninety-minute sleep cycle, 8% in the second, 10% in the third, 12% in the fourth, and 13–14% in the fifth. Most of this awake time occurred shortly after REM sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AkerstedtEtAl2002_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-AkerstedtEtAl2002-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, many humans wake up with an <a href="/wiki/Alarm_clock" title="Alarm clock">alarm clock</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RonnenbergEtAl2007_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-RonnenbergEtAl2007-21">[21]</a></sup> however, people can also reliably wake themselves up at a specific time with no need for an alarm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AkerstedtEtAl2002_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-AkerstedtEtAl2002-20">[20]</a></sup> Many sleep quite differently on workdays versus days off, a pattern which can lead to chronic circadian desynchronization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaterhouseEtAl2012_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaterhouseEtAl2012-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RonnenbergEtAl2007_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-RonnenbergEtAl2007-21">[21]</a></sup> Many people regularly look at television and other screens before going to bed, a factor which may exacerbate disruption of the circadian cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChangAM_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChangAM-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Scientific studies on sleep have shown that sleep stage at awakening is an important factor in amplifying <a href="/wiki/Sleep_inertia" title="Sleep inertia">sleep inertia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timing">Timing</span></h2>
<p>Sleep timing is controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Circadian_clock" title="Circadian clock">circadian clock</a> (Process C), sleep-wake <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> (Process S), and to some extent by the individual will.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circadian_clock">Circadian clock</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">Circadian rhythm</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm_sleep_disorder" title="Circadian rhythm sleep disorder">Circadian rhythm sleep disorder</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:404px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biological_clock_human.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Biological_clock_human.svg/402px-Biological_clock_human.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Biological_clock_human.svg/603px-Biological_clock_human.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Biological_clock_human.svg/804px-Biological_clock_human.svg.png 2x" width="402"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biological_clock_human.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The human "<a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">biological clock</a>"</div></div></div>
<p>Sleep timing depends greatly on <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormonal</a> signals from the circadian clock, or Process C, a complex neurochemical system which uses signals from an organism's environment to recreate an internal day–night rhythm. Process C counteracts the homeostatic drive for sleep during the day (in <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a> animals) and augments it at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FullerEtAl2006_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FullerEtAl2006-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaterhouseEtAl2012_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-WaterhouseEtAl2012-22">[22]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Suprachiasmatic_nucleus" title="Suprachiasmatic nucleus">suprachiasmatic nucleus</a> (SCN), a brain area directly above the <a href="/wiki/Optic_chiasm" title="Optic chiasm">optic chiasm</a>, is presently considered the most important nexus for this process; however, secondary clock systems have been found throughout the body.
</p><p>An organism whose circadian clock exhibits a regular rhythm corresponding to outside signals is said to be <i><a href="/wiki/Entrainment_(chronobiology)" title="Entrainment (chronobiology)">entrained</a></i>; an entrained rhythm persists even if the outside signals suddenly disappear. If an entrained human is isolated in a bunker with constant light or darkness, he or she will continue to experience rhythmic increases and decreases of body temperature and melatonin, on a period that slightly exceeds 24 hours. Scientists refer to such conditions as <a href="/wiki/Free-running_sleep" title="Free-running sleep">free-running</a> of the circadian rhythm. Under natural conditions, light signals regularly adjust this period downward, so that it corresponds better with the exact 24 hours of an Earth day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RonnenbergEtAl2007_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-RonnenbergEtAl2007-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkEdgar1999_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-DijkEdgar1999-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The circadian clock exerts constant influence on the body, affecting <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sinusoidal</a> oscillation of <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">body temperature</a> between roughly 36.2 °C and 37.2 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkEdgar1999_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-DijkEdgar1999-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CzeislerWright1999_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-CzeislerWright1999-29">[29]</a></sup> The suprachiasmatic nucleus itself shows conspicuous oscillation activity, which intensifies during subjective day (i.e., the part of the rhythm corresponding with daytime, whether accurately or not) and drops to almost nothing during subjective night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZlomanczukSchwartz1999_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZlomanczukSchwartz1999-30">[30]</a></sup> The circadian pacemaker in the suprachiasmatic nucleus has a direct neural connection to the <a href="/wiki/Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland">pineal gland</a>, which releases the hormone <a href="/wiki/Melatonin" title="Melatonin">melatonin</a> at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZlomanczukSchwartz1999_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZlomanczukSchwartz1999-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">Cortisol</a> levels typically rise throughout the night, <a href="/wiki/Cortisol_awakening_response" title="Cortisol awakening response">peak in the awakening hours</a>, and diminish during the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanCauterSpiegel1999_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-VanCauterSpiegel1999-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wehr1999_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wehr1999-31">[31]</a></sup> Circadian <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">prolactin</a> secretion begins in the late afternoon, especially in women, and is subsequently augmented by sleep-induced secretion, to peak in the middle of the night. Circadian rhythm exerts some influence on the nighttime secretion of growth hormone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanCauterSpiegel1999_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-VanCauterSpiegel1999-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The circadian rhythm influences the ideal timing of a restorative sleep episode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RonnenbergEtAl2007_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-RonnenbergEtAl2007-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyatt1999_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wyatt1999-32">[32]</a></sup> Sleepiness increases during the night. REM sleep occurs more during body temperature minimum within the circadian cycle, whereas <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep</a> can occur more independently of circadian time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkEdgar1999_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-DijkEdgar1999-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The internal circadian clock is profoundly influenced by changes in light, since these are its main clues about what time it is. Exposure to even small amounts of light during the night can suppress melatonin secretion, and increase body temperature and wakefulness. Short pulses of light, at <a href="/wiki/Phase_response_curve" title="Phase response curve">the right moment</a> in the circadian cycle, can significantly 'reset' the internal clock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CzeislerWright1999_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-CzeislerWright1999-29">[29]</a></sup> Blue light, in particular, exerts the strongest effect,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaterhouseEtAl2012_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-WaterhouseEtAl2012-22">[22]</a></sup> leading to concerns that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_media_and_sleep" title="Electronic media and sleep">electronic media use</a> before bed may interfere with sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChangAM_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChangAM-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern humans often find themselves desynchronized from their internal circadian clock, due to the requirements of work (especially <a href="/wiki/Shift_work" title="Shift work">night shifts</a>), long-distance travel, and the influence of universal indoor lighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkEdgar1999_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-DijkEdgar1999-28">[28]</a></sup> Even if they have sleep debt, or feel sleepy, people can have difficulty staying asleep at the peak of their circadian cycle. Conversely, they can have difficulty waking up in the trough of the cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AkerstedtEtAl2002_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-AkerstedtEtAl2002-20">[20]</a></sup> A healthy young adult entrained to the sun will (during most of the year) fall asleep a few hours after sunset, experience body temperature minimum at 6 a.m., and wake up a few hours after sunrise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkEdgar1999_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-DijkEdgar1999-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Process_S">Process S</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_debt" title="Sleep debt">Sleep debt</a></div>
<p>Generally speaking, the longer an organism is awake, the more it feels a need to sleep ("sleep debt"). This driver of sleep is referred to as <b>Process S</b>. The balance between sleeping and waking is regulated by a process called <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>. Induced or perceived lack of sleep is called <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">sleep deprivation</a>.
</p><p>Process S is driven by the depletion of <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> and accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">adenosine</a> in the forebrain that disinhibits the <a href="/wiki/Ventrolateral_preoptic_nucleus" title="Ventrolateral preoptic nucleus">ventrolateral preoptic nucleus</a>, allowing for inhibition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascending_reticular_activating_system" title="Ascending reticular activating system">ascending reticular activating system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep deprivation tends to cause slower brain waves in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frontal_cortex" title="Frontal cortex">frontal cortex</a>, shortened attention span, higher anxiety, impaired memory, and a grouchy mood. Conversely, a well-rested organism tends to have improved memory and mood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Neurophysiological and functional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imaging_studies" title="Imaging studies">imaging studies</a> have demonstrated that frontal regions of the brain are particularly responsive to homeostatic sleep pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is disagreement on how much sleep debt is possible to accumulate, and whether sleep debt is accumulated against an individual's average sleep or some other benchmark. It is also unclear whether the prevalence of sleep debt among adults has changed appreciably in the <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">industrialized world</a> in recent decades. Sleep debt does show some evidence of being cumulative. Subjectively, however, humans seem to reach maximum sleepiness 30 hours after waking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkEdgar1999_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-DijkEdgar1999-28">[28]</a></sup> It is likely that in <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western societies</a>, children are sleeping less than they previously have.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>One neurochemical indicator of sleep debt is <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">adenosine</a>, a neurotransmitter that inhibits many of the bodily processes associated with wakefulness. Adenosine levels increase in the cortex and basal forebrain during prolonged wakefulness, and decrease during the sleep-recovery period, potentially acting as a homeostatic regulator of sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25175972_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25175972-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">Coffee</a>, tea, and other sources of <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a> temporarily block the effect of adenosine, prolong sleep latency, and reduce total sleep time and quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_timing">Social timing</span></h3>
<p>Humans are also influenced by aspects of <i>social time</i>, such as the hours when other people are awake, the hours when work is required, the time on clocks, etc. <a href="/wiki/Time_zone" title="Time zone">Time zones</a>, standard times used to unify the timing for people in the same area, correspond only approximately to the natural rising and setting of the sun. An extreme example of the approximate nature of time zones is China, a country which used to span five time zones and now <a href="/wiki/Time_in_China" title="Time in China">officially uses only one</a> (UTC+8).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RonnenbergEtAl2007_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-RonnenbergEtAl2007-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h3>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphasic_sleep" title="Polyphasic sleep">polyphasic sleep</a>, an organism sleeps several times in a 24-hour cycle, whereas in monophasic sleep this occurs all at once. Under experimental conditions, humans tend to alternate more frequently between sleep and wakefulness (i.e., exhibit more polyphasic sleep) if they have nothing better to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DijkEdgar1999_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-DijkEdgar1999-28">[28]</a></sup> Given a 14-hour period of darkness in experimental conditions, humans tended towards bimodal sleep, with two sleep periods concentrated at the beginning and at the end of the dark time. Bimodal sleep in humans was more common before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wehr1999_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wehr1999-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different characteristic sleep patterns, such as the familiarly so-called "<a href="/wiki/Lark_(person)" title="Lark (person)">early bird</a>" and  "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Night_owl_(person)" title="Night owl (person)">night owl</a>", are called <i><a href="/wiki/Chronotype" title="Chronotype">chronotypes</a></i>. Genetics and sex have some influence on chronotype, but so do habits. Chronotype is also liable to change over the course of a person's lifetime. Seven-year-olds are better disposed to wake up early in the morning than are fifteen-year-olds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaterhouseEtAl2012_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-WaterhouseEtAl2012-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RonnenbergEtAl2007_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-RonnenbergEtAl2007-21">[21]</a></sup> Chronotypes far outside the normal range are called circadian rhythm sleep disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Naps">Naps</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nap" title="Nap">Nap</a></div><p>Naps are short periods of sleep that one might take during the daytime, often in order to get the necessary amount of rest. Napping is often associated with childhood, but around one-third of American adults partake in it daily. The optimal nap duration is around 10-20 minutes, as researchers have proven that it takes at least 30 minutes to enter slow-wave sleep, the deepest period of sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-41">[41]</a></sup> Napping too long and entering the slow wave cycles can make it difficult to awake from the nap and leave one feeling unrested. This period of drowsiness is called <a href="/wiki/Sleep_inertia" title="Sleep inertia">sleep inertia</a>.
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal,_Cusco,_Per%C3%BA,_2015-07-31,_DD_49.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5514" data-file-width="8230" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_49.JPG/220px-Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_49.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_49.JPG/330px-Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_49.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_49.JPG/440px-Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_49.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hombre_echando_una_siesta_en_San_Crist%C3%B3bal,_Cusco,_Per%C3%BA,_2015-07-31,_DD_49.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Man napping in San Cristobal, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Siesta" title="Siesta">siesta</a> habit has recently been associated with a 37% lower coronary mortality, possibly due to reduced cardiovascular stress mediated by daytime sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naska_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naska-42">[42]</a></sup> Short naps at mid-day and mild evening exercise were found to be effective for improved sleep, cognitive tasks, and mental health in elderly people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<p>Monozygotic (identical) but not dizygotic (fraternal) twins tend to have similar sleep habits. Neurotransmitters, molecules whose production can be traced to specific genes, are one genetic influence on sleep that can be analyzed. The circadian clock has its own set of genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Genes which may influence sleep include <a href="/wiki/ABCC9" title="ABCC9">ABCC9</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DEC2" title="DEC2">DEC2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor_D2" title="Dopamine receptor D2">Dopamine receptor D2</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Molecular_Genetics_of_Human_Sleep_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Molecular_Genetics_of_Human_Sleep-45">[45]</a></sup> and variants near <a href="/wiki/PAX8" title="PAX8">PAX 8</a> and <a href="/wiki/VRK2" title="VRK2">VRK2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27494321_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27494321-46">[46]</a></sup> While the latter have been found in a <a href="/wiki/Genome-wide_association_study" title="Genome-wide association study">GWAS study</a> that primarily detects correlations (but not necessarily causation), other genes have been shown to have a more direct effect. For instance, mice lacking <a href="/wiki/Dihydropyrimidine_dehydrogenase_(NADP%2B)" title="Dihydropyrimidine dehydrogenase (NADP+)">dihydropyrimidine dehydrogenase</a> (Dpyd) had 78.4 min less sleep during the lights-off period than wild-type mice. Dpyd encodes the rate-limiting <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> in the metabolic pathway that catabolizes <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thymidine" title="Thymidine">thymidine</a> to β-<a href="/wiki/Alanine" title="Alanine">alanine</a>, an inhibitory <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a>. This also supports the role of β-alanine as a neurotransmitter that promotes sleep in mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality">Quality</span></h3>
<p>The quality of sleep may be evaluated from an objective and a subjective point of view. Objective sleep quality refers to how difficult it is for a person to fall asleep and remain in a sleeping state, and how many times they wake up during a single night. Poor sleep quality disrupts the cycle of transition between the different stages of sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Subjective sleep quality in turn refers to a sense of being rested and regenerated after awaking from sleep. A study by A. Harvey et al. (2002) found that insomniacs were more demanding in their evaluations of sleep quality than individuals who had no sleep problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Homeostatic sleep propensity (the need for sleep as a function of the amount of time elapsed since the last adequate sleep episode) must be balanced against the circadian element for satisfactory sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zisapel_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zisapel-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Along with corresponding messages from the circadian clock, this tells the body it needs to sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-52">[52]</a></sup> The timing is correct when the following two circadian markers occur after the middle of the sleep episode and before awakening:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyatt1999_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wyatt1999-32">[32]</a></sup> maximum concentration of the hormone melatonin, and minimum core body temperature.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideal_duration">Ideal duration</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1090" data-file-width="1480" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg/220px-Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg/330px-Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg/440px-Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Effects_of_sleep_deprivation.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main health effects of <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">sleep deprivation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> indicating impairment of normal maintenance by sleep</div></div></div>
<p>Human sleep-needs vary by age and amongst individuals;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> sleep is considered to be adequate when there is no daytime sleepiness or dysfunction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Moreover, self-reported sleep duration is only moderately correlated with actual sleep time as measured by <a href="/wiki/Actigraphy" title="Actigraphy">actigraphy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and those affected with <a href="/wiki/Sleep_state_misperception" title="Sleep state misperception">sleep state misperception</a> may typically report having slept only four hours despite having slept a full eight hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-healthcom_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-healthcom-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers have found that sleeping 6–7 hours each night correlates with longevity and cardiac health in humans, though many underlying factors may be involved in the causality behind this relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27494321_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27494321-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep difficulties are furthermore associated with psychiatric disorders such as <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thase2006_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thase2006-66">[66]</a></sup> Up to 90 percent of adults with depression are found to have sleep difficulties. Dysregulation detected by EEG includes disturbances in sleep continuity, decreased delta sleep and altered REM patterns with regard to latency, distribution across the night and density of eye movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep duration can also vary according to season. Up to 90% of people report longer sleep duration in winter, which may lead to more pronounced <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_affective_disorder" title="Seasonal affective disorder">seasonal affective disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg/220px-WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg/330px-WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg/440px-WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WLA_metmuseum_Bronze_statue_of_Eros_sleeping_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze statue of <a href="/wiki/Eros" title="Eros">Eros</a> sleeping, 3rd century BC–early 1st century AD</div></div></div>
<p>By the time infants reach the age of two, their brain size has reached 90 percent of an adult-sized brain;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahl_2009_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl_2009-70">[70]</a></sup> a majority of this brain growth has occurred during the period of life with the highest rate of sleep. The hours that children spend asleep influence their ability to perform on cognitive tasks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenni_Dahl_2008_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenni_Dahl_2008-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scher_2005_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scher_2005-72">[72]</a></sup> Children who sleep through the night and have few night waking episodes have higher cognitive attainments and easier temperaments than other children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scher_2005_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scher_2005-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spruyt_2007_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spruyt_2007-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernier_2010_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernier_2010-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep also influences language development. To test this, researchers taught infants a faux language and observed their recollection of the rules for that language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hupbach_2009_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hupbach_2009-75">[75]</a></sup> Infants who slept within four hours of learning the language could remember the language rules better, while infants who stayed awake longer did not recall those rules as well. There is also a relationship between infants' vocabulary and sleeping: infants who sleep longer at night at 12 months have better vocabularies at 26 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernier_2010_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernier_2010-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Children can greatly benefit from a structured bedtime routine. This can look differently among families, but will generally consist of a set of rituals such as reading a bedtime story, a bath, brushing teeth, and can also include a show of affection from the parent to the child such a hug or kiss before bed. A bedtime routine will also include a consistent time that the child is expected to be in bed ready for sleep. Having a reliable bedtime routine can help improve a child's quality of sleep as well as prepare them to make and keep healthy sleep hygiene habits in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hours_of_sleep">Hours of sleep</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2251" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg/220px-%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg/330px-%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg/440px-%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22Plenty_of_sleep_keeps_him_on_the_job%22_-_NARA_-_514792.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World War II poster issued by the US government</div></div></div><p>Children need many hours of sleep per day in order to develop and function properly: up to 18 hours for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newborn" title="Newborn">newborn</a> babies, with a declining rate as a child ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-52">[52]</a></sup> Early in 2015, after a two-year study,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sleepfoundation_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-sleepfoundation-77">[77]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/National_Sleep_Foundation" title="National Sleep Foundation">National Sleep Foundation</a> in the US announced newly revised recommendations as shown in the table below.
</p><table class="wikitable">
