<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions">Functions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration">Restoration</span></h3>
<p>The human organism physically restores itself during sleep, occurring mostly during <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep</a> during which body temperature, heart rate, and brain oxygen consumption decrease. In both the brain and body, the reduced rate of <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> enables countervailing restorative processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CespuglioEtAl2005_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-CespuglioEtAl2005-78">[78]</a></sup> The brain requires sleep for restoration, whereas these processes can take place during quiescent waking in the rest of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The essential function of sleep may be its restorative effect on the brain: "Sleep is of the brain, by the brain and for the brain."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hobson2005_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hobson2005-80">[80]</a></sup> This theory is strengthened by the fact that sleep is observed to be a necessary behavior across most of the animal kingdom, including some of the least evolved animals which have no need for other functions of sleep, such as memory consolidation or dreaming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joiner2016_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Joiner2016-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>While awake, brain metabolism generates <a href="/wiki/Metabolism#Energy_from_organic_compounds" title="Metabolism">end products</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a>, which may be damaging to brain cells and inhibit their proper function. During sleep, metabolic rates decrease and reactive oxygen species generation is reduced, enabling restorative processes. The sleeping brain has been shown to remove metabolic end products at a faster rate than during an awake state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xie2013_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xie2013-81">[81]</a></sup> The mechanism for this removal appears to be the <a href="/wiki/Glymphatic_system" title="Glymphatic system">glymphatic system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xie2013_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xie2013-81">[81]</a></sup> Sleep may facilitate the synthesis of molecules that help repair and protect the brain from metabolic end products generated during waking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabolic" title="Anabolic">Anabolic</a> hormones, such as <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormones</a>, are secreted preferentially during sleep. The brain concentration of <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> increases during sleep, and is depleted through metabolism during wakefulness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CespuglioEtAl2005_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-CespuglioEtAl2005-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The effect of sleep duration on <a href="/wiki/Somatic_(biology)" title="Somatic (biology)">somatic</a> growth is not completely known. One study recorded growth, height, and weight, as correlated to parent-reported time in bed in 305 children over a period of nine years (age 1–10). It was found that "the variation of sleep duration among children does not seem to have an effect on growth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">Slow-wave sleep</a> affects <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormone</a> levels in adult men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanCauterSpiegel1999_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-VanCauterSpiegel1999-12">[12]</a></sup> During eight hours of sleep, men with a high percentage of <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep (SWS)</a> (average 24%) also had high growth hormone secretion, while subjects with a low percentage of SWS (average 9%) had low growth hormone secretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memory_processing">Memory processing</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_and_memory" title="Sleep and memory">Sleep and memory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience_of_sleep" title="Neuroscience of sleep">Neuroscience of sleep</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sleep_and_learning" title="Sleep and learning">Sleep and learning</a></div>
<p>It has been widely accepted that sleep must support the formation of long-term memory, and generally increasing previous learning and experiences recalls. However, its benefit seems to depend on the phase of sleep and the type of memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> For example, declarative and procedural memory-recall tasks applied over early and late nocturnal sleep, as well as wakefulness controlled conditions, have been shown that declarative memory improves more during early sleep (dominated by SWS) while procedural memory during late sleep (dominated by REM sleep) does so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:13-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>With regard to declarative memory, the functional role of SWS has been associated with hippocampal replays of previously encoded neural patterns that seem to facilitate long-term memory consolidation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:13-87">[87]</a></sup> This assumption is based on the active system consolidation hypothesis, which states that repeated reactivations of newly encoded information in the hippocampus during slow oscillations in NREM sleep mediate the stabilization and gradual integration of declarative memory with pre-existing knowledge networks on the cortical level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> It assumes the hippocampus might hold information only temporarily and in a fast-learning rate, whereas the neocortex is related to long-term storage and a slow-learning rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-:02-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-:13-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-:52-91">[91]</a></sup> This dialogue between the hippocampus and neocortex occurs in parallel with hippocampal <a href="/wiki/Sharp_waves_and_ripples" title="Sharp waves and ripples">sharp-wave ripples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sleep_spindle" title="Sleep spindle">thalamo-cortical spindles</a>, synchrony that drives the formation of the spindle-ripple event which seems to be a prerequisite for the formation of long-term memories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-:13-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-:52-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-:32-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reactivation of memory also occurs during wakefulness and its function is associated with serving to update the reactivated memory with newly encoded information, whereas reactivations during SWS are presented as crucial for memory stabilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-:13-87">[87]</a></sup> Based on targeted memory reactivation (TMR) experiments that use associated memory cues to triggering memory traces during sleep, several studies have been reassuring the importance of nocturnal reactivations for the formation of persistent memories in neocortical networks, as well as highlighting the possibility of increasing people's memory performance at declarative recalls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-:02-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-:22-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-:52-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-:32-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, nocturnal reactivation seems to share the same neural oscillatory patterns as reactivation during wakefulness, processes which might be coordinated by <a href="/wiki/Theta_wave" title="Theta wave">theta activity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-94">[94]</a></sup> During wakefulness, theta oscillations have been often related to successful performance in memory tasks, and cued memory reactivations during sleep have been showing that theta activity is significantly stronger in subsequent recognition of cued stimuli as compared to uncued ones, possibly indicating a strengthening of memory traces and lexical integration by cuing during sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> However, the beneficial effect of TMR for memory consolidation seems to occur only if the cued memories can be related to prior knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dreaming">Dreaming</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">Dream</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glimpse_of_a_dream_(9391068364).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Glimpse_of_a_dream_%289391068364%29.jpg/220px-Glimpse_of_a_dream_%289391068364%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Glimpse_of_a_dream_%289391068364%29.jpg/330px-Glimpse_of_a_dream_%289391068364%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Glimpse_of_a_dream_%289391068364%29.jpg/440px-Glimpse_of_a_dream_%289391068364%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glimpse_of_a_dream_(9391068364).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dreams often feel like waking life, yet with added surrealism.</div></div></div><p>During sleep, especially REM sleep, humans tend to experience dreams. These are elusive and mostly unpredictable first-person experiences which seem logical and realistic to the dreamer while they are in progress, despite their frequently bizarre, irrational, and/or surreal qualities that become apparent when assessed after waking. Dreams often seamlessly incorporate concepts, situations, people, and objects within a person's mind that would not normally go together. They can include apparent sensations of all types, especially vision and movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HobsonEtAl2000_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-HobsonEtAl2000-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dreams tend to rapidly fade from memory after waking. Some people choose to keep a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dream_journal" title="Dream journal">dream journal</a>, which they believe helps them build dream recall and facilitate the ability to experience <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucid_dreams" title="Lucid dreams">lucid dreams</a>.
</p><p>People have proposed many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotheses" title="Hypotheses">hypotheses</a> about the functions of dreaming. <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> postulated that dreams are the symbolic expression of frustrated desires that have been relegated to the <a href="/wiki/Unconscious_mind" title="Unconscious mind">unconscious mind</a>, and he used <a href="/wiki/Dream_interpretation" title="Dream interpretation">dream interpretation</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">psychoanalysis</a> in attempting to uncover these desires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Counterintuitively, <a href="/wiki/Nocturnal_penile_tumescence" title="Nocturnal penile tumescence">penile erections during sleep</a> are not more frequent during sexual dreams than during other dreams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Parasympathetic_nervous_system" title="Parasympathetic nervous system">parasympathetic nervous system</a> experiences increased activity during REM sleep which may cause erection of the penis or clitoris. In males, 80% to 95% of REM sleep is normally accompanied by partial to full penile erection, while only about 12% of men's dreams contain sexual content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin_2012_537_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin_2012_537-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Allan_Hobson" title="Allan Hobson">John Allan Hobson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_McCarley" title="Robert McCarley">Robert McCarley</a> propose that dreams are caused by the random firing of neurons in the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a> during the REM period. Neatly, this theory helps explain the irrationality of the mind during REM periods, as, according to this theory, the <a href="/wiki/Forebrain" title="Forebrain">forebrain</a> then creates a <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">story</a> in an attempt to reconcile and make sense of the nonsensical sensory information presented to it. This would explain the odd nature of many dreams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HobsonMcCarley1977_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-HobsonMcCarley1977-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disorders">Disorders</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insomnia">Insomnia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">Insomnia</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Psychological_stress_and_sleep" title="Psychological stress and sleep">Psychological stress and sleep</a></div>
<p>Insomnia is a general term for difficulty falling asleep and/or staying asleep. Insomnia is the most common sleep problem, with many adults reporting occasional insomnia, and 10–15% reporting a chronic condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Insomnia can have many different causes, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychological_stress_and_Sleep" title="Psychological stress and Sleep">psychological stress</a>, a poor sleep environment, an inconsistent sleep schedule, or excessive mental or physical stimulation in the hours before bedtime. Insomnia is often treated through behavioral changes like keeping a regular sleep schedule, avoiding stimulating or stressful activities before bedtime, and cutting down on stimulants such as caffeine. The sleep environment may be improved by installing heavy drapes to shut out all sunlight, and keeping computers, televisions, and work materials out of the sleeping area.
</p><p>A 2010 review of published scientific research suggested that exercise generally improves sleep for most people, and helps sleep disorders such as insomnia. The optimum time to exercise <i>may</i> be 4 to 8 hours before bedtime, though exercise at any time of day is beneficial, with the exception of heavy exercise taken shortly before bedtime, which may disturb sleep. However, there is insufficient evidence to draw detailed conclusions about the relationship between exercise and sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Sleeping medications such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambien" title="Ambien">Ambien</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunesta" title="Lunesta">Lunesta</a> are an increasingly popular treatment for insomnia. Although these nonbenzodiazepine medications are generally believed to be better and safer than earlier generations of sedatives, they have still generated some controversy and discussion regarding side effects. <a href="/wiki/White_noise" title="White noise">White noise</a> appears to be a promising treatment for <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obstructive_sleep_apnea">Obstructive sleep apnea</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Obstructive_sleep_apnea" title="Obstructive sleep apnea">Obstructive sleep apnea</a> is a condition in which major pauses in breathing occur during sleep, disrupting the normal progression of sleep and often causing other more severe health problems. Apneas occur when the muscles around the patient's airway relax during sleep, causing the airway to collapse and block the intake of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Obstructive sleep apnea is more common than <a href="/wiki/Central_sleep_apnea" title="Central sleep apnea">central sleep apnea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> As oxygen levels in the blood drop, the patient then comes out of deep sleep in order to resume breathing. When several of these episodes occur per hour, sleep apnea rises to a level of seriousness that may require treatment.
</p><p>Diagnosing sleep apnea usually requires a professional sleep study performed in a sleep clinic, because the episodes of wakefulness caused by the disorder are extremely brief and patients usually do not remember experiencing them. Instead, many patients simply feel tired after getting several hours of sleep and have no idea why. Major risk factors for sleep apnea include chronic fatigue, old age, obesity, and snoring.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aging_and_sleep">Aging and sleep</span></h3>
<p>People over age 60 with prolonged sleep (8–10 hours or more; average sleep duration of 7–8 hours in the elderly) have a 33% increased risk of all-cause <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality</a> and 43% increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>, while those with short sleep (less than 7 hours) have a 6% increased risk of all-cause mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silva_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-silva-107">[107]</a></sup> Sleep disorders, including <a href="/wiki/Sleep_apnea" title="Sleep apnea">sleep apnea</a>, insomnia, or periodic limb movements, occur more commonly in the elderly, each possibly impacting sleep quality and duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silva_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-silva-107">[107]</a></sup> A 2017 review indicated that older adults do not need less sleep, but rather have an impaired ability to obtain their sleep needs, and may be able to deal with sleepiness better than younger adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mander_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-mander-108">[108]</a></sup> Various practices are recommended to mitigate sleep disturbances in the elderly, such as having a light bedtime snack, avoidance of <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a>, daytime naps, excessive evening stimulation, and <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> products, and using regular bedtime and wake schedules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlp_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-mlp-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_disorders">Other disorders</span></h3>
<p>Sleep disorders include <a href="/wiki/Narcolepsy" title="Narcolepsy">narcolepsy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Periodic_limb_movement_disorder" title="Periodic limb movement disorder">periodic limb movement disorder</a> (PLMD), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Restless_leg_syndrome" title="Restless leg syndrome">restless leg syndrome</a> (RLS), <a href="/wiki/Upper_airway_resistance_syndrome" title="Upper airway resistance syndrome">upper airway resistance syndrome</a> (UARS), and the circadian rhythm sleep disorders. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatal_familial_insomnia" title="Fatal familial insomnia">Fatal familial insomnia</a>, or FFI, an extremely rare genetic disease with no known treatment or cure, is characterized by increasing insomnia as one of its symptoms; ultimately sufferers of the disease stop sleeping entirely, before dying of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeoMay2010_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeoMay2010-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somnambulism" title="Somnambulism">Somnambulism</a>, known as sleepwalking, is a sleeping disorder, especially among children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep_health">Sleep health</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_hygiene" title="Sleep hygiene">Sleep hygiene</a></div>
<p>Low quality sleep has been linked with health conditions like <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a>. While poor sleep is common among those with cardiovascular disease, some research indicates that poor sleep can be a contributing cause. Short sleep duration of less than seven hours is correlated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_heart_disease" title="Coronary heart disease">coronary heart disease</a> and increased risk of death from coronary heart disease. Sleep duration greater than nine hours is also correlated with coronary heart disease, as well as <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> and cardiovascular events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hale_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hale-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cespedes_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cespedes-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27647451_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27647451-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In both children and adults, short sleep duration is associated with an increased risk of obesity, with various studies reporting an increased risk of 45–55%. Other aspects of sleep health have been associated with obesity, including daytime napping, sleep timing, the variability of sleep timing, and low sleep efficiency. However, sleep duration is the most-studied for its impact on obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hale_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hale-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep problems have been frequently viewed as a symptom of mental illness rather than a causative factor. However, a growing body of evidence suggests that they are both a cause and a symptom of mental illness. Insomnia is a significant predictor of <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">major depressive disorder</a>; a <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> of 170,000 people showed that insomnia at the beginning of a study period indicated a more than the twofold increased risk for major depressive disorder. Some studies have also indicated correlation between insomnia and <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>. Sleep disorders can increase the risk of <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">psychosis</a> and worsen the severity of psychotic episodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hale_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hale-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep research also displays differences in race and class. Short sleep and poor sleep are observed more frequently in ethnic minorities than in whites. African-Americans report experiencing short durations of sleep five times more often than whites, possibly as a result of social and environmental factors.  Black children and children in disadvantaged neighborhoods have much higher rates of sleep apnea than white children and respond more poorly to treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep health can be improved through implementing good <a href="/wiki/Sleep_hygiene" title="Sleep hygiene">sleep hygiene</a> habits. Having good sleep hygiene can help to improve your physical and mental health by providing your body with the necessary rejuvenation only restful sleep can provide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Some ways to improve sleep health include going to sleep at consistent times every night, avoiding any electronic devices such as televisions in the bedroom, getting adequate exercise throughout your day, and avoiding caffeine in the hours before going to sleep. Another way to greatly improve sleep hygiene is by creating a peaceful and relaxing sleep environment. Sleeping in a dark and clean room with things like a white noise maker can help facilitate restful sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Drugs_and_diet">Drugs and diet</span></h2>
<p>Drugs which induce sleep, known as <a href="/wiki/Hypnotic" title="Hypnotic">hypnotics</a>, include <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a>, although these interfere with REM;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sleep_medicine_a04_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-sleep_medicine_a04-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nonbenzodiazepine" title="Nonbenzodiazepine">Nonbenzodiazepine</a> hypnotics such as <a href="/wiki/Eszopiclone" title="Eszopiclone">eszopiclone</a> (Lunesta), <a href="/wiki/Zaleplon" title="Zaleplon">zaleplon</a> (Sonata), and <a href="/wiki/Zolpidem" title="Zolpidem">zolpidem</a> (Ambien); <a href="/wiki/Antihistamine" title="Antihistamine">antihistamines</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Diphenhydramine" title="Diphenhydramine">diphenhydramine</a> (Benadryl) and <a href="/wiki/Doxylamine" title="Doxylamine">doxylamine</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol (ethanol)</a>, despite its <a href="/wiki/Rebound_effect" title="Rebound effect">rebound effect</a> later in the night and interference with REM;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sleep_medicine_a04_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-sleep_medicine_a04-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barbiturate" title="Barbiturate">barbiturates</a>, which have the same problem; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melatonin_(medication)" title="Melatonin (medication)">melatonin</a>, a component of the circadian clock, and released naturally at night by the <a href="/wiki/Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland">pineal gland</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TurekCzeisler1999_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-TurekCzeisler1999-121">[121]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>, which may also interfere with REM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stimulant" title="Stimulant">Stimulants</a>, which inhibit sleep, include <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Adenosine" title="Adenosine">adenosine</a> antagonist; <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/MDMA" title="MDMA">MDMA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empathogen-entactogen" title="Empathogen-entactogen">empathogen-entactogens</a>, and related drugs; <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>, which can alter the circadian rhythm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Methylphenidate" title="Methylphenidate">methylphenidate</a>, which acts similarly; and other <a href="/wiki/Analeptic" title="Analeptic">analeptic</a> drugs like <a href="/wiki/Modafinil" title="Modafinil">modafinil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armodafinil" title="Armodafinil">armodafinil</a> with poorly understood mechanisms. Consuming high amounts of the stimulant caffeine can result in interrupted sleep patterns and sometimes sleep deprivation. This vicious cycle can result in drowsiness which can then result in a higher consumption of caffeine in order to stay awake the next day. This cycle can lead to decreased cognitive function and an overall feeling of fatigue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dietary and nutritional choices may affect sleep duration and quality. One 2016 review indicated that a high-<a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> diet promoted a shorter onset to sleep and a longer duration of sleep than a high-fat diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-an-126">[126]</a></sup> A 2012 investigation indicated that mixed <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macronutrient" title="Macronutrient">macronutrients</a> are needed to promote quality sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> A varied diet containing fresh fruits and vegetables, low <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Whole_grain" title="Whole grain">whole grains</a> may be optimal for individuals seeking to improve sleep quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-an-126">[126]</a></sup> High-quality <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trials</a> on long-term dietary practices are needed to better define the influence of diet on sleep quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-an-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropology">Anthropology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2081" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg/220px-Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg/330px-Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg/440px-Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pieter_Bruegel_d._%C3%84._037.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Land_of_Cockaigne_(Bruegel)" title="The Land of Cockaigne (Bruegel)">The Land of Cockaigne</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder" title="Pieter Bruegel the Elder">Pieter Bruegel the Elder</a>, 1567</div></div></div>
<p>Research suggests that sleep patterns vary significantly across cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthman_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worthman-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeon_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeon-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samson_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Samson-130">[130]</a></sup> The most striking differences are observed between societies that have plentiful sources of artificial light and ones that do not. The primary difference appears to be that pre-light cultures have more broken-up sleep patterns. For example, people without artificial light might go to sleep far sooner after the sun sets, but then wake up several times throughout the night, punctuating their sleep with periods of wakefulness, perhaps lasting several hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthman_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Worthman-128">[128]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Pre-industrial_society" title="Pre-industrial society">pre-industrial</a> <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biphasic_and_polyphasic_sleep" title="Biphasic and polyphasic sleep">biphasic (bimodal) sleeping</a> was considered the norm. Sleep onset was determined not by a set bedtime, but by whether there were things to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>The boundaries between sleeping and waking are blurred in these societies. Some observers believe that nighttime sleep in these societies is most often split into two main periods, the first characterized primarily by deep sleep and the second by REM sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthman_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Worthman-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some societies display a fragmented sleep pattern in which people sleep at all times of the day and night for shorter periods. In many <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> societies, people sleep on and off throughout the day or night depending on what is happening. Plentiful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_light" title="Artificial light">artificial light</a> has been available in the industrialized West since at least the mid-19th century, and sleep patterns have changed significantly everywhere that lighting has been introduced. In general, people sleep in a more concentrated burst through the night, going to sleep much later, although this is not always the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthman_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-Worthman-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A._Roger_Ekirch" title="A. Roger Ekirch">A. Roger Ekirch</a> thinks that the traditional pattern of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Segmented_sleep" title="Segmented sleep">segmented sleep</a>," as it is called, began to disappear among the urban upper class in Europe in the late 17th century and the change spread over the next 200 years; by the 1920s "the idea of a first and second sleep had receded entirely from our social consciousness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-133">[133]</a></sup> Ekirch attributes the change to increases in "street lighting, domestic lighting and a surge in coffee houses," which slowly made nighttime a legitimate time for activity, decreasing the time available for rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-133">[133]</a></sup> Today in most societies people sleep during the night, but in very hot climates they may sleep during the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>, many Muslims sleep during the day rather than at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some societies, people sleep with at least one other person (sometimes many) or with animals. In other cultures, people rarely sleep with anyone except for an intimate partner. In almost all societies, sleeping partners are strongly regulated by social standards. For example, a person might only sleep with the <a href="/wiki/Immediate_family" title="Immediate family">immediate family</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a>, a spouse or romantic partner, children, children of a certain age, children of a specific gender, peers of a certain gender, friends, peers of equal social rank, or with no one at all. Sleep may be an actively social time, depending on the sleep groupings, with no constraints on noise or activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthman_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-Worthman-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>People sleep in a variety of locations. Some sleep directly on the ground; others on a skin or blanket; others sleep on platforms or <a href="/wiki/Bed" title="Bed">beds</a>. Some sleep with blankets, some with pillows, some with simple headrests, some with no head support. These choices are shaped by a variety of factors, such as climate, protection from predators, housing type, technology, personal preference, and the incidence of pests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthman_128-5"><a href="#cite_note-Worthman-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_mythology_and_literature">In mythology and literature</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seven_sleepers_(Menologion_of_Basil_II).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="746" data-file-width="1076" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Seven_sleepers_%28Menologion_of_Basil_II%29.jpg/290px-Seven_sleepers_%28Menologion_of_Basil_II%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Seven_sleepers_%28Menologion_of_Basil_II%29.jpg/435px-Seven_sleepers_%28Menologion_of_Basil_II%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Seven_sleepers_%28Menologion_of_Basil_II%29.jpg/580px-Seven_sleepers_%28Menologion_of_Basil_II%29.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seven_sleepers_(Menologion_of_Basil_II).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medieval <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">manuscript illumination</a> from the <i><a href="/wiki/Menologion_of_Basil_II" title="Menologion of Basil II">Menologion of Basil II</a></i> (985 AD), showing the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sleepers" title="Seven Sleepers">Seven Sleepers of Ephesus</a> sleeping in their cave</div></div></div>
<p>Sleep has been seen in culture as similar to death since antiquity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-William-136">[136]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypnos" title="Hypnos">Hypnos</a> (the god of sleep) and <a href="/wiki/Thanatos" title="Thanatos">Thanatos</a> (the god of death) were both said to be the children of <a href="/wiki/Nyx" title="Nyx">Nyx</a> (the goddess of night).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-William-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">John Donne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Percy Bysshe Shelley</a>, John Keats and other poets have all written poems about the relationship between sleep and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-William-136">[136]</a></sup> Shelley describes them as "both so passing, strange and wonderful!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_136-3"><a href="#cite_note-William-136">[136]</a></sup> Keats similarly poses the question: "Can death be sleep, when life is but a dream".<a class="external autonumber" href="https://www.poemhunter.com/poem/on-death-13/" rel="nofollow">[1]</a> Many people consider dying in one's sleep is the most peaceful way to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_136-4"><a href="#cite_note-William-136">[136]</a></sup> Phrases such as "big sleep" and "<a href="/wiki/Rest_in_peace" title="Rest in peace">rest in peace</a>" are often used in reference to death,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_136-5"><a href="#cite_note-William-136">[136]</a></sup> possibly in an effort to lessen its finality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William_136-6"><a href="#cite_note-William-136">[136]</a></sup> Sleep and dreaming have sometimes been seen as providing the potential for visionary experiences. In medieval Irish tradition, in order to become a <a href="/wiki/Fil%C3%AD" title="Filí">filí</a>, the poet was required to undergo a ritual called the <i><a href="/wiki/Imbas_forosnai" title="Imbas forosnai">imbas forosnai</a></i>, in which they would enter a mantic, trancelike sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacKillop_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacKillop-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many cultural stories have been told about people falling asleep for extended periods of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burstein_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein-140">[140]</a></sup> The earliest of these stories is the ancient Greek legend of <a href="/wiki/Epimenides" title="Epimenides">Epimenides of Knossos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saint_Rip_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saint_Rip-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> According to the biographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Laërtius">Diogenes Laërtius</a>, Epimenides was a shepherd on the Greek island of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-144">[144]</a></sup> One day, one of his sheep went missing and he went out to look for it, but became tired and fell asleep in a cave under <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ida_(Crete)" title="Mount Ida (Crete)">Mount Ida</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-144">[144]</a></sup> When he awoke, he continued searching for the sheep, but could not find it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-144">[144]</a></sup> so he returned to his old farm, only to discover that it was now under new ownership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild_144-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-144">[144]</a></sup> He went to his hometown, but discovered that nobody there knew him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-6"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup> Finally, he met his younger brother, who was now an old man,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-7"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild_144-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-144">[144]</a></sup> and learned that he had been asleep in the cave for fifty-seven years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-8"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothschild_144-5"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>A far more famous instance of a "long sleep" today is the Christian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legendary_material_in_Christian_hagiography" title="Legendary material in Christian hagiography">legend</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sleepers" title="Seven Sleepers">Seven Sleepers of Ephesus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-9"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup> in which seven Christians flee into a cave during pagan times in order to escape <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecution</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-10"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup> but fall asleep and wake up 360 years later to discover, to their astonishment, that the Roman Empire is now predominantly Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_139-11"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-139">[139]</a></sup> The American author <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a>'s short story "<a href="/wiki/Rip_Van_Winkle" title="Rip Van Winkle">Rip Van Winkle</a>", first published in 1819 in his collection of short stories <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sketch_Book_of_Geoffrey_Crayon,_Gent." title="The Sketch Book of Geoffrey Crayon, Gent.">The Sketch Book of Geoffrey Crayon, Gent.</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burstein_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> is about a man in <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">colonial America</a> named Rip Van Winkle who falls asleep on one of the <a href="/wiki/Catskill_Mountains" title="Catskill Mountains">Catskill Mountains</a> and wakes up twenty years later after the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burstein_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein-140">[140]</a></sup> The story is now considered one of the greatest classics of <a href="/wiki/American_literature" title="American literature">American literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burstein_140-3"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_art">In art</span></h3>
<p>Of the thematic representations of sleep in art, physician and sleep researcher Meir Kryger wrote, "[Artists] have intense fascination with mythology, dreams, religious themes, the parallel between sleep and death, reward, abandonment of conscious control, healing, a depiction of innocence and serenity, and the erotic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostart_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostart-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>

"Sleep duration from infancy to adolescence: reference values and generational trends". <i>Pediatrics</i>. <b>111</b> (2): 302–7. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1542%2Fpeds.111.2.302" rel="nofollow">10.1542/peds.111.2.302</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12563055" rel="nofollow">12563055</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:8727836" rel="nofollow">8727836</a>. <q>Thus, the shift in the evening bedtime across cohorts accounted for the substantial decrease in sleep duration in younger children between the 1970s and the 1990s... [A] more liberal parental attitude toward evening bedtime in the past decades is most likely responsible for the bedtime shift and for the decline of sleep duration...</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Pediatrics&amp;rft.atitle=Sleep+duration+from+infancy+to+adolescence%3A+reference+values+and+generational+trends&amp;rft.volume=111&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=302-7&amp;rft.date=2003-02&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A8727836%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F12563055&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1542%2Fpeds.111.2.302&amp;rft.aulast=Iglowstein&amp;rft.aufirst=I&amp;rft.au=Jenni%2C+OG&amp;rft.au=Molinari%2C+L&amp;rft.au=Largo%2C+RH&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASleep"></span></span>
